This core problem is brought about by some essential factors such as planning, implementation mechanisms, and procedures involved in fiscal management.

**Limited technical know-how in program budgeting.** One of the seen challenges in the implementation and fund utilization for the first three years of BARMM operation is the limited technical know-how in program budgeting. The BARMM adopts the national policy of cash-based budgeting, limiting the coverage of obligation to one fiscal year only, which enjoins government institutions to be more proactive in their expenditures through optimized planning and ensuring of implementation readiness of programs and projects. Understandably, the BARMM MOAs are only starting to build the foundation of its respective organization. Nevertheless, impactful results and service delivery may only be achieved through improvement in the efficiency in program budgeting.

**Manual budget processing.** Due to the advancement of technology, manual budget processing has been a problem in different organizations nowadays. Limited IT infrastructure, no new government accounting system (e-NGAs), budget and treasury management system (BTMS), unified reporting system (URS), and lack of IT experts on automated budget processing are some of its roots.

There is yet an established system for government accounting or cash management which may have some effect in the delayed implementation of programs and projects. This problem, however, is multi-faceted as it can be attributed to the lack of an efficient IT infrastructure, low connectivity, and expertise in terms of system development. Most notably, the lack of catch-up plan and insufficient capacity of program implementers in the face of natural and man-made calamities contribute greatly to the low absorptive capacity and flexibility of the implementing BARMM MOAs.

**Lack of fiscal and monetary policy.** Policies are relevant to organizations as it guides its operation. However, lack of fiscal and monetary policy has been a problem in the region as up until now there is no revenue and investment code, this might be due to lack of expert adept at BARMM context, lack of prioritization, and lack of an appropriate mechanism that would enable LGUs to generate locally sourced revenue.

Lack of expertise and awareness of Islamic finance is also a factor that can be attributed to the lack of intervention to promote financial literacy (on financial and monetary policies), poor information dissemination on financial literacy and inclusion, and less local public investment.

**Fluctuating rates in local revenue generation.** Although the collections for regional fees and charges have continuously increased in 2021, the collection in regional wealth tax and contractors has decreased, thus making the collection performance short by five percent. The restrictions from the COVID-19 pandemic affected the procurement of infrastructure projects, thus, what was disbursed during the year was mainly from the mobilization fees and some contracts have not been awarded. Thus, the collection of contractors' taxes was not maximized. The collection of regional wealth tax on the other hand is dependent on the production of mines. This type of collection is not stable due to the varying amount of mining production.

**Weak planning, programming, and budgeting linkages.** There is seemingly fragmented planning, programming, and budgeting linkages. This is manifested through the lack of mechanisms like a joint committee or body composed of the planning and financial offices, absence of long-term strategic plans, and limited convergence interventions. This is further weakened by the fast turnover of employees and slow hiring process. Another is the limited technical budgeting capacity due to changes in financial practitioners.

**Delayed implementation of PPAs.** There are several factors that hinder the timely implementation of PPAs which include unpreparedness for internal and external adversities due to lack of catch-up plan, overlapping of implementation schedule of several PPAs, limited technical capacity on program implementation, inadequate staffing and weak structure, and slow hiring process.

Improving the cash management is another concern caused by lack of an automatic reversion system (treasury). The MDS used by the national government in disbursement of funds is a good government practice that is yet to be exercised in the BARMM. Moreover, exploratory talks have been kickstarted with the government servicing bank which is aimed at improving the cash management of the region.

Lastly, the delayed submission of reportorial documents also contributes to the disparity in actual expenditure and monitoring records. The regional government is expected to consolidate reportorial requirements to streamline financial data needed for the presentation of a credible financial position.

### Multi-stakeholder Engagement

The following are the key challenges of multi-stakeholder engagement to be addressed in 2023-2028:

**Differences in approaches and strategies in governance of the political leaders.** There have been these differences in approaches and strategies in governance of leaders because of their differences in political creed and affiliation.

**Poor inclusivity and access to information.** It is being observed that the whole-of-society approach is not adopted due to lack of policy on Rules of Engagement and non-recognition of the expertise of non-state actors. There is also limited public access to information on government processes due to poor implementation of freedom of information (FOI).

### Bangsamoro Justice System

Strengthening the multi-justice system in the BARMM is crucial to ensure inclusive coverage among the diverse communities in the region. The BARMM is composed of a culturally diverse population which requires the Bangsamoro government to implement an inclusive justice system.

Under RA 11054, "The Parliament shall adopt measures to ensure mutual respect and protection of the distinct beliefs, customs, and traditions of the Bangsamoro people and the other inhabitants in the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region." The BOL guarantees the protection of the rights of the people in the region.

The following are the key challenges of the BJS to be addressed in 2023-2028:

**BJS not fully implemented in the BARMM.** As provided in Article X of the BOL, the BJS shall be administered in accordance with the unique cultural and historical heritage of the Bangsamoro and shall be in consonance with the Constitution, Shari'ah, traditional or tribal laws, and other relevant laws. The Bangsamoro government is yet to establish and operationalize the BJS structure in the region.

**Lack of Shari'ah judges and personnel for Shari'ah courts.** In 2019, the JBC has created 48 SCC under the jurisdiction of the SDC in the BARMM. While only 33 of the SCC are organized, and 15 are not yet organized which are situated in Tawi-Tawi and Basilan provinces. There is a necessity of establishing additional Shari'ah courts within and outside the region. During the BJS subsector consultation, it was mentioned that the reason why the Supreme Court did not establish additional Shari'ah courts is because of the lesser statistics of cases recorded. Another factor contributing to the lack of Shari'ah judges and personnel for Shari'ah Courts is the inorganization of the Integrated Shari'ah Bar of the Philippines.

**Less prioritization on Shari'ah capacity development.** There is less priority on capacity development on Shari'ah resulting to lesser number of capacitated Shari'ah practitioners. Also, the lack of training hampered the gradual appointment of qualified Shari'ah judges.

Presently, the National Commission on Muslim Filipinos (NCMF) is the only recognized institution by the Supreme Court of the Philippines to facilitate training on Shari'ah subjects which is included in the requirements for taking the Special Shari'ah Bar Examination. However, the NCMF training is very limited in the number of slots for their accommodation. Meanwhile, being a college graduate with a bachelor's degree in Shari'ah is not sufficient for taking the special bar examination as they also need to undergo training from the NCMF to secure a certificate from the Supreme Court.

The Supreme Court through the Office of the Bar Confidant conducts the Special Shari'ah Bar Examination only once every two years upon the request of the Secretary/ Chief Executive Officer of the NCMF.

As a result, there is inadequate pool of highly specialized and qualified Shari'ah practitioners in the BARMM to fill in the vacant plantilla positions in the Shari'ah courts.

**Slow implementation of BOL provision on BJS.** Reiterated in Title V, Section 12B of the BAA 13, Shari'ah Public Assistance Office will be headed by a director assisted by two counselors who are members of the Philippine Shari'ah Bar and they shall be assisted by an immediate staff. The director and two Shari'ah counselors shall be appointed by the Chief Minister. The Shari'ah Public Assistance Office is mandated to provide free legal assistance to poor or indigent party litigants.

Currently, the Shari'ah Public Assistance Office has not been fully established and operationalized. No resolutions to facilitate the immediate organization of the office that would primarily provide free legal assistance to indigent party litigants; prepare and file petitions, complaints, and/or responsive pleadings, and represent indigent defendants in ordinary civil actions, special civil actions, special proceedings and in any stage criminal proceedings before the Shari'ah Courts.

The same applied to the Office for Traditional Justice System. As per Section 27, D of the BAA 13, the Office for Traditional Justice system will be the primary responsible for overseeing the study, preservation, and development of traditional or tribal justice systems within the BARMM and it shall be attached to the OCM. Up to this date, these offices have not yet been brought before the Bangsamoro Parliament.

Full implementation of these laws will significantly increase access to the justice system and swift resolution of disputes, especially among the poor and marginalized groups. In return, it will increase the trust and confidence of the public. Greater access to justice comes with immense benefits for societies, including improving social cohesion and people's well-being.

**Absence of BTA Legislation on the implementation of the Bangsamoro Justice System.** The absence of policy issuance or support for the complementation of the multi-judicial system of the Bangsamoro may cause confusion to the people in determining the applicable laws to their concerns, given the multi-racial occurrence in the BARMM.

The multi-judicial system in the Bangsamoro is composed of the Shari'ah, Alternative Dispute Resolution (ADR), Customary Laws, Tribal, and Regular Courts. The inadequacy in the awareness of this multi-judicial system leads to a very low public trust in the justice system that may cause community disorder and conflicts. Poor people tend to put the law into their own hands and refuse to elevate the case to the proper court.

In the case of ADR, there are no funds allocated for the Council of Elders to motivate them in resolving disputes. As a result, they are not interested in the arising altercations in their surroundings which lead to serious conflicts. Moreover, the Council of Elders is not properly identified to preserve their status in their community.

**Weak coordination among the five pillars of the justice system: Law enforcement, prosecution, courts, correction, community.** The BARMM needs a strengthened five pillars of the Criminal Justice System (CJS) for a just, inclusive, and accessible Philippine judicial system. The Criminal Justice System (CJS) shall consist of a series of actions, tasks, or activities that will constitute the formal response to perceived law-and-order issues: crime prevention and reduction; the arrest and prosecution of suspects; and the hearing of criminal cases by the courts.

According to the assessment of PDP 2023-2028, despite the positive developments, there are still lacking in the policy reforms and key legislations. The CJS in the Philippines is composed of five pillars - law enforcement, prosecution, the courts, correction, and the community. It was found out in the PDP that the fragmentation of the CJS remains a challenge; backlogs in resolving cases, delays caused by inefficient practices, and aging persist; limited resources weaken the justice sector; and low public confidence in the justice system undermines the rule of law. Also, there is a possibility of undue administration of justice such as the absence of preliminary investigation, untimely or no delivery of subpoena, no arraignment, and other due processes.

These problems mainly affect the implementation of programs and strengthening coordination. In the BARMM, the surging number of crimes keep on flashing on the news, but the authorities would only say that the proper investigation will be done. What the people want to see is minimal crime incidence. The prompt action of the authorities in the repression of criminals must be strengthened. The coordination among pillars of the justice system shall be strengthened to oversee the implementation of strict monitoring of the progress of the cases and imposing penalties on erring lawyers.

The court pillar plays a major role in the resolution of cases. They shall carefully evaluate the information and pieces of evidence before pursuing a hearing and rendering a final judgment. In this pillar, the number of cases also affects the functions of the judges such as the speedy trial. This requires sufficient facilities and an increased number of judges to facilitate the adjudication of cases. Furthermore, the court shall strengthen its coordination among other pillars for the proper administration of justice.

The necessity of strengthening the administration of justice to prevent injustices in the future is also crucial. There are cases that reached the Supreme Court petitioning for the grave abuse of discretion of the judges. Hence, a policy strengthening the coordination and monitoring mechanism for the administration of justice is indispensable.

The first to uphold the law is the law enforcement pillar. It represents public authority and is in charge of preserving public safety and order. This pillar must remain strong in order for the populace to be responsive and involved in achieving due process. The BARMM is responsible for closely monitoring law enforcement activities and enforcing sanctions for improper use of force.

There is a real pushback in the correction pillar. The rampant crimes in the BARMM could be an implication of the failure of reformation and rehabilitation of the offenders. The Bangsamoro government shall provide support to the programs and projects for reformation and rehabilitation in preventing recidivism cases. Additionally, those found guilty of ta'azir charges, which are minor infractions punishable by Shari'ah courts, must be imprisoned in separate institutions while serving their terms in order to avoid further influence from those with major offenses and to ensure their prompt reformation.

The LGUs as part of the community pillar plays a major role in the development of the justice system in the Bangsamoro. The MILG-BARMM as its counterpart is mandated to promote peace and order, ensure public safety, and further strengthen local government capability aimed toward the effective delivery of basic services to the citizenry. Through LGUs, the Bangsamoro government can contribute to easy access of the poor or vulnerable groups to justice.

Also, poor litigants' access to available legal services may be hampered by a lack of awareness on their part. The costs incurred during hearings and other legal proceedings are another common factor that contributes to injustices.

Furthermore, there should be a mechanism for inter-agency dialogue and coordinated implementation of reforms in the justice system.

---

## Strategic Framework

### Development Goal

**Sectoral Goal:** Stable, Just, and Accountable Bangsamoro Government

### Sectoral and Sub-Sector Objectives:

**1. Effective and efficient bureaucracy established.**

Sub-sector objectives under this target are:
- Seamless delivery of public service strengthened;
- Local government administration and supervision enhanced;
- Ideal functionality and performance of LGUs achieved; and
- Intergovernmental relations mechanisms, transparency and accountability mechanisms, and the Bangsamoro Civil Service strengthened.

**2. Development planning processes and systems enhanced.**

Sub-sector objectives under this target are:
- Responsive Bangsamoro development and thematic plans ensured;
- Vertical and horizontal planning and budgeting linkages strengthened;
- Data and communications management strengthened; and
- M&E system institutionalized and strengthened.

**3. Responsive and efficient legislative services ensured.**

Sub-sector objectives under this target are:
- Responsive and efficient Parliament Legislative services;
- Enactment of remaining priority codes and other legislative measures ensured;
- Adequate BTA Human and Physical Resources provided;
- Committee systems and processes enhanced; and
- Parliament's engagement enhanced.

**4. Public expenditure and revenue generation improved.**

Sub-sector objectives under this target are:
- Responsive and efficient utilization of funds ensured;
- Automated budget management systems established;
- Enabling fiscal policy environment strengthened; and
- Resource mobilization efforts improved.

**5. Inclusive and open governance improved.**

Sub-sector objectives under this target are:
- Multi-stakeholder engagement broadened;
- Institutional arrangements on development initiatives strengthened;
- E-governance strengthened; and
- Feedback mechanism strengthened.

**6. People-centered Bangsamoro justice system improved.**

Sub-sector objectives under this target are:
- Bangsamoro Justice System structures in the BARMM established and operationalized;
- Coordination mechanism between the Supreme Court of the Philippines and the Bangsamoro government institutionalized;
- Qualified Shari'ah judges and personnel for Shari'ah courts ensured;
- Substantive laws pertaining to the BJS enacted;
- Effective remedy, education, training, and public awareness on promoting basic human rights implemented; and
- Coordination among the five pillars of Justice system strengthened.

---

## Priority Strategies and Programs

### Strategies

The Bangsamoro government through its Development Administration will achieve the following strategies for the realization of a stable, just, and accountable Bangsamoro government:

### Bureaucracy

**Strategy 1:** Strengthen the collaboration among the national, regional, and constituent LGUs through the establishment of comprehensive guidelines that clearly defines roles, functions, and services on different levels of governance;

**Strategy 2:** Leverage the use of new technology to strengthen vertical and horizontal coordination-collaboration for effective delivery of basic services;

**Strategy 3:** Reinforce existing National government policies relevant to BARMM bureaucracy by intensifying policy promotion and strengthening the organizational mechanisms for transparency, and accountability measures;

**Strategy 4:** Rationalize regional agencies and local offices to ensure complementation or avoid overlapping and duplication of functions;

**Strategy 5:** Strengthen administration and supervision over LGUs by strengthening linkages between the regional and local governments, and strengthening internal capacity of LGUs through establishment of government centers and other development facilities, and enhancement of functionality and performance of LGUs to 7 elements of functionality;

**Strategy 6:** Fill in local government mandatory positions in accordance with the Bangsamoro civil service system/local government code; enhance and strengthen participation of LGUs to local special bodies; and strengthen participation of private sector and CSO representatives in local governance;

**Strategy 7:** Establish fully functional National Government - Bangsamoro Government Intergovernmental Relations Bodies such as the Philippine Congress-Bangsamoro Parliament Forum; Intergovernmental Fiscal Policy Board; Joint Body for the Zones of Joint Cooperation; Intergovernmental Infrastructure Development Board; Intergovernmental Energy Board; Bangsamoro Sustainable Development Board; and Council of Leaders;

**Strategy 8:** Intensify the investment in Human Resource and improve the Human Resource Management (HRM) systems and processes through the implementation of the Program to Institutionalize Meritocracy and Excellence in Human Resource Management (PRIME-HRM); and

**Strategy 9:** Increase awareness on civil service values and norms (patriotism, integrity, excellence, and spirituality) among all government personnel in BARMM.

### Development Planning

**Strategy 1:** Strengthen planning and budget links through operationalization of planning structures and adoption of synchronized planning and budget policy;

**Strategy 2:** Ensure the vertical and horizontal alignment and integration of plans;

**Strategy 3:** Ensure availability and accessibility of evidence-based data for the formulation of relevant plans and policies, establishment of regional statistical system, and systematized data collection and management;

**Strategy 4:** Develop, localize, and disseminate IEC materials on planning and development;

**Strategy 5:** Ensure the readiness of identified priority programs and projects in the development plans;

**Strategy 6:** Establish Bangsamoro Library and Records Center, and develop Standardized Documents Management System; and

**Strategy 7:** Strengthen Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E) through the establishment of standardized systems and mechanisms, institutionalization of policies, capacity building and database management system.

### Legislation

**Strategy 1:** Ensure holistic, comprehensive and inclusive parliamentary policies responsive to the needs of the Bangsamoro through investment on research and development, and multi-stakeholder engagement;

**Strategy 2:** Ensure BTA physical structure is constructed, competitive hiring, and adequate capacity building for effective and efficient Parliament; and

**Strategy 3:** Strengthen Bangsamoro Parliament and Congress coordination through the operationalization of BTA-Congress Forum to ensure complementation of national and regional laws and policies.

### Fiscal Management

**Strategy 1:** Establishment and institutionalization of Public Financial Management Mechanisms;

**Strategy 2:** Improving asset management and monitoring efficiency;

**Strategy 3:** Digitization of budget management systems;

**Strategy 4:** Increase promotion and literacy on Islamic finance and banking;

**Strategy 5:** Streamline formulation of fiscal and monetary policies;

**Strategy 6:** Enhancement of collection mechanisms;

**Strategy 7:** Streamline initiatives for LGU coordination on financial affairs; and

**Strategy 8:** Strengthening revenue generation through exploration and utilization of natural resources.

### Multi-Stakeholder Engagement

**Strategy 1:** Broaden institutional, inter-governmental arrangements and multi-stakeholders' engagement through whole-of-government and whole-of-society approaches for participative policy development, planning, program implementation, and monitoring and evaluation; and

**Strategy 2:** Ensure public access to transparent, strategic and risk communication, and consultative mechanism.

### Bangsamoro Justice System

**Strategy 1:** Strengthen the Bangsamoro Justice System by implementing the institutional provisions under the BOL; enactment of substantive laws on Shari'ah; ensure BARMM representation in the Judicial and Bar Council; and increasing the number of operational/functional Shari'ah Courts with judges and personnel;

**Strategy 2:** Establish an effective coordination mechanism between the Supreme Court and the Bangsamoro Government; as well as between the five pillars of the Philippine justice system to improve the delivery and accessibility of services to the Bangsamoro;

**Strategy 3:** Intensify information and educational campaign on Shari'ah and basic human rights through public symposium, trainings, consultation, inclusion in the basic curriculum; offering Shari'ah scholarship program, and special courses for executives; and institutionalizing the Shari'ah related activities in the different offices and LGUs in the BARMM as part of the annual report;

**Strategy 4:** Ensure capacity development by activating the Shari'ah Academy in cooperation with the NCMF as training institution in relation to the Supreme Court-administered Special Shari'ah Bar Examinations; and

**Strategy 5:** Enactment of legislation on alternative dispute resolution and identification of focal office for the BJS.

---

## Priority Programs

### Bureaucracy

**Policy Development Program.** This program aims to operationalize the strategy to strengthen the collaboration among the national, regional, and constituent LGUs through the establishment of comprehensive guidelines that clearly define roles, functions, and services on different levels of governance.

It intends to achieve a strengthened seamless delivery of public service by establishing clear and comprehensive guidelines in their roles, functions, and services on the different levels of government to avoid unnecessary conflicts and misunderstandings in the performance of its functions and mandates.

The projects under this program are: (1) Formulation of Policy Guidelines on strengthening the linkages of ministries, offices & agencies; and, (2) Research Policy Formulation and Implementation.

**Supervising and Incentivizing Good Local Governance Performance Program.** This program intends to operationalize the strategy on strengthening administration and supervision over LGUs; strengthen linkages between the regional and local governments; and enhancement of functionality and performance of LGUs to 7 elements of functionality.

The projects under this program are: (1) Seal of Good Local Governance (SGLG) for B/LGUs; Salamat Hashim Excellence Award for Leadership (SEAL); (2) Lupong Tagapamayapa Incentives Award (LTIA); (3) LGU Grant Assistance for Innovative Practices (LGAIP); and (4) Search for Model Barangays.

**Local Government Facilities Development Program.** This program intends to operationalize strategies for strengthening the internal capacity of LGUs through the establishment of government centers and other development facilities, which will lead to the enhancement of functionality and performance of LGUs to 7 elements of functionality. This Program aims that every B/LGU will have complete government centers with facilities to reach the ideal provision of basic services to Bangsamoro communities.

The projects under this program are: (1) Support to Local Moral Governance development facilities, and (2) Capacity development for B/LGUs on the operationalization and maintenance of development facilities.

**Bangsamoro Local Economic Support Services (BLESS) Program.** This program intends to operationalize strategies for strengthening corporate capacity of LGUs through the establishment of the Local Economic Enterprise, which will lead to the enhancement of functionality and performance of LGUs to 7 elements of functionality. This Program aims for LGUs to have self-sustaining income-generating local economic enterprises to spur local economic growth, promote economic activities, and generate job opportunities for their constituents.

The projects under this program are: (1) Support to Local Government's Economic Enterprise; and (2) Capacity development on business management.

**Localizing E-Governance on Accelerated Provision for Services (LeAPS) Program.** The LeAPS is envisioned to become an integral part of the delivery of basic services of the LGU as well as the regional government to accelerate and improve data and information management through Information Technology and Communication. It intends to operationalize the strategies on leveraging the use of new technology to strengthen vertical and horizontal coordination and collaboration for the effective delivery of basic services which is intended to achieve the sectoral objective of establishing effective and efficient bureaucracy.

The projects under this program are: (1) E-Service on Digital Bangsamoro Portal with devLIVE+; and (2) Policy Development Support on Digital Transformation.

**Transforming and Empowering Local Governance Program.** This program is geared towards transforming and empowering local governments which aims to operationalize both strategies such as Strategy 5: strengthening administration and supervision over LGUs; strengthening linkages between the regional and local governments; and enhancement of functionality and performance of LGUs to 7 elements of functionality; and Strategy 6: filling in local government mandatory positions in accordance with the Bangsamoro civil service system/local government code; enhance and strengthen the participation of LGUs to local special bodies; and strengthen the participation of private sector and CSO representatives in local governance through capacitating LGUs towards transformational and empowering local governance for the LGUs, Local Special Bodies, and Council to effectively discharge their duties and responsibilities; and to strengthen the collaboration and participation of the Civil Society to governance program.

The projects under this program are: (1) Revenue Enhancement Assistance for LGU (REAL); (2) Harnessing Organizational Proficiency & Effectiveness (HOPE) for LGUs (Social Governance); (3) Engaging the SK, Empowering the Youth (ESKEY); (4) Tamang Alituntunin at Hakbang Alay ng Responsableng Kaagapay (TAHARA); and (5) Newly Elected Officials.

### Development Planning

**Environment, Land Use and Urban Development Program.** This program intends to enable and empower LGUs under BARMM to formulate, craft, update and review their Comprehensive Land Use Plans, an instrument for the local government unit to allocate available land resources to different sectors of its territory for different functions. This further helps the local government to avoid creating duplicate programs and unplanned placement of projects in their jurisdictions.

It also provides mitigation and risk assessment that will help the LGUs in identifying hazards and risk prone areas due to calamities and unprecedented events due to Climate Change.

**BARMM Development Communications Program.** This program intends to operationalize the strategy of ensuring the availability and accessibility of evidence-based data for the formulation of relevant plans and policies, establishment of regional statistical systems, and systematized data collection and management.

Under this program, the projects include: (1) Development of BARMM Strategic Communications Plan; and (2) Standardization of BARMM Information, Education and Communication (IEC) Materials.

### Fiscal Management

**Expenditure Management Program.** This program streamlines initiatives to improve allocative efficiency, program budgeting, and fiscal monitoring in the region. It aims to introduce policies, procedures, and mechanisms that will enhance the regional institutions' expenditure management to increase fund utilization and absorptive capacity.

This program intends to operationalize Strategy 1 under Fiscal Management and institutionalization of Public Financial Management Mechanisms and adheres to the sub-sector objective on the responsive and efficient utilization of funds ensured. Furthermore, this program is responsive to the region's priority agenda on Stronger BARMM Bureaucracy, specifically on enhancing enabling policies that impel transparency, accountability, and improvement of overall public financial management.

Under this Program, notable projects include (1) Regional and Local Expenditure Management Enhancement; (2) Organization and Productivity Enhancement; and (3) Financial Transparency and Performance Management Enhancement.

### Bangsamoro Justice System

**Shari'ah Counselors at Law Development Program.** This program aspires to operationalize the strategy on ensuring capacity development by activating the Shari'ah Academy in cooperation with the NCMF as training institution in relation to the Supreme Court-administered Special Shari'ah Bar Examinations.

It intends to achieve an effective remedy, education, training, and public awareness on promoting basic human rights; inclusive open governance and ensuring qualified Shari'ah Judges and personnel for Shari'ah courts.

This program consists of the following projects: (1) The Shari'ah Academy and, (2) Shari'ah Practitioners Development Project.

**Bangsamoro Justice Institutional Strengthening Program.** This program intends to initiate the implementation of Institutional provisions under the Bangsamoro Organic Law; the enactment of the substantive laws on Shari'ah; BARMM representation in the Judicial and Bar Council; and increase the number of Operational/Functional Shari'ah Courts with judges and personnel.

This program tends to achieve the sectoral objectives of the enactment of the substantive laws pertaining to the BJS; establishing and operationalizing the BJS structures in the BARMM; ensuring qualified Shari'ah Judges and personnel for Shari'ah Courts; and people-centered BJS is improved.

This program consists of projects which are: (1) BOL Institutionalization project and, (2) Shari'ah Institutionalization Project.

**Bangsamoro Justice System Educational and Awareness Program.** This program intends to intensify information and educational campaigns on Shari'ah and basic human rights through public symposiums, training, consultation, and inclusion in the basic curriculum; offering Shari'ah scholarship program, and special courses for executives; and institutionalizing the Shari'ah related activities in the different offices and LGUs in the BARMM as part of the annual report.

The projects under this program are: (1) BJS Awareness Campaign Project; and, (2) Shari'ah Mainstreaming Project.

---

## Priority Policy and Legislative Agenda

**Bangsamoro Revenue Code.** This shall cover taxing powers of the Bangsamoro Government, in accordance with the Constitution and the BOL.

**Bangsamoro Investment Code.** This shall cover the matters relating to investments and incentives regulations that will be applied in the Bangsamoro Region.

**Bangsamoro Budget System Act.** This shall provide for the creation of the Bangsamoro Budget Office (BBO) under the control and supervision of the MFBM, and is primarily mandated to promote the sound, efficient, and effective management, and utilization of all resources of the Bangsamoro Government.

**Bangsamoro Islamic Banking and Finance.** This covers matters pertaining to legal and regulatory framework for Islamic Banking and Finance in Bangsamoro, recognizes the vital role of Islamic banking and finance in creating opportunities for greater financial inclusion, and discusses this system of finance based on Islamic law.

**Bangsamoro Local Governance Code.** This shall provide for a more responsive and accountable local government structure instituted through a system of decentralization whereby constituent local government units shall be given more powers, authority, responsibilities, and resources in a gradual and systematic approach.

This policy agenda intends to support sub-sector objectives on strengthening the delivery of public service; and strengthening and enhancing local government administration and supervision; as well as in achieving the ideal functionality and performance of LGUs.

**Bangsamoro Electoral Code.** This code is proposed to be promulgated to prescribe the structural, functional, and procedural principles in the elections of officers within the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region, and secure honest, credible, just, and free elections thereby allowing democratic political participation and reflecting the genuine will of the electorate.

**Enactment of Bangsamoro Code of Muslim Personal Laws.** This priority legislative agenda tends to enforce and harmonize the application of laws in all Shari'ah Courts in the Philippines. This will pave way in establishing and operationalizing the structures of BJS in the BARMM and will set in motion an institutionalized coordination mechanism between the Supreme Court of the Philippines and the Bangsamoro government.

Presidential Decree 1083 provides for the general enactment on Administration of Muslim Personal Laws. The Code of Muslim Personal Laws (CMPL) refers to the law that governs the family and personal relations of the Muslim Filipinos. This decree recognizes the legal system of Muslims in the Philippines as part of the law of the land, codifies Muslim personal laws, and provides for the administration and enforcement of the Code through the establishment of Shari'ah courts.

**Codification of ADR mechanisms in resolving tribal disputes.** The Codification of the ADR will pave way to the use of the alternative dispute system as well as the establishment or appointment of focal office to implement the ADR. ADR has emerged as the key to decongesting court dockets. It's a process or procedure used to resolve a dispute or controversy, other than by adjudication of a presiding judge of a court or an officer of a government agency in which a neutral third party participates to assist in the resolution of issues. There are three types of alternative dispute resolution mechanisms: conciliation, mediation, and arbitration incorporating provisions that will strengthen the partnership among BARMM MOAs and other non-state actors.

**An enabling law for open governance.** Inspired by the Open Government Partnership initiative, the concept of Bangsamoro open governance is committed to the principles of inclusive participation, promoting transparency, fighting corruption, empowering the Bangsamoro people and leveraging on technology to strengthen local moral governance. The values of transparency and open government is crucial to make government information more accessible and available through the use of technology to ensure accountability.

This policy agenda intends to support the sub-sector objective of strengthening transparency and accountability mechanisms for a more inclusive and open participation in governmental processes.

Fiscal openness will provide the platform to inform the Bangsamoro people and local and international partners on regional and local governance, maximize engagement and establish citizen feedback mechanisms to help fight corruption.

**Strengthen the standardization of monitoring and evaluation mechanism of the Bangsamoro Government and Local Development Council of BARMM LGUs.** The standardized M&E mechanism shall provide a scheme for the Bangsamoro government and Local Development Council in monitoring and evaluation of development programs or projects in the BARMM.

**Bangsamoro Government Communications Policy.** This encompasses the central objectives and principles for the Bangsamoro government's communication with its constituents, civil society organizations, academe, businesses, and other stakeholders. The principles of the policy will serve as the basis for all the BARMM MOAs on how communication will operate within government departments and across the regional government, mainland, and island provinces.

This policy agenda intends to support the sub-sectoral objective of strengthening data and communications management. Moreover, it shall serve as a framework in the development of communication plans and strategies for the government.

**Creation of Bangsamoro Human Settlements Adjudication Commission.** The Commission shall assume and continue to perform the adjudication functions of the former HLURB-ARMM, now Ministry of Human Settlements and Development (MHSD) - BARMM, and shall be attached to the Ministry for policy, planning and program coordination only. In the performance of its functions, the Commission shall be guided by the Constitutional provision that affords all persons the right to a speedy disposition of their cases before all judicial, quasi-judicial or administrative bodies. Toward this end, the independence of the adjudication process and the speedy resolution of disputes involving housing and real estate development and transactions, land use, and HOAs within BARMM jurisdiction shall be ensured, and all parties to these controversies shall be accorded equal access to legal processes.

---

# Chapter 6: Mainstreaming of Bangsamoro Cultural Diversity, Beliefs, Heritage, and Identity

Section 24, Article IX of RA 11054 known as the BOL provides that the Bangsamoro government shall preserve the history, culture, arts, traditions, and the rich cultural heritage of the Bangsamoro people and their Sultanates, such as those of Sulu, Maguindanao, Kabuntalan, Buayan, the Royal House of Ranao and of the Iranun, and the non-Moro indigenous peoples of the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region.

Pursuant to the said provision of the BOL, the Bangsamoro government identified Bangsamoro culture, heritage, identity, and diversity as the twelfth priority of the enhanced 12-Point Priority Agenda of the BARMM. This chapter aims to contribute in Agenda No. 7 of the 8-Point Socioeconomic Agenda of the Marcos Administration, and SDGs 10, 11, and 17.

This Chapter discusses the current situation, priority challenges, and opportunities for the preservation and promotion of identity and diverse culture in BARMM. It also sets forth the goal, objectives, strategies, and priority interventions laid out by the Bangsamoro government for the next six years. This features the unique culture and identity of the BARMM.

---

## Sectoral Assessment

### Sectoral Trends

**Bangsamoro culture and identity recognized.** The term Moro is a diverse category, with great variation culturally and linguistically, as well as in religiosity and the adherence to Islamic teachings (Paredes, 2015). It was initially a derogatory term applied by the Spanish colonizers who battled the 'Moors' in the Iberian Peninsula - modern Spain and Portugal, implemented aggressive anti-Muslim policies.

The Maguindanaon, Meranao, Iranun or Iranaun, Tausug, Yakan, and other cultural groups retain distinct traditions and identities, their shared historical identification with Islam and a shared history of being targeted by colonial and post-colonial state persecution (ibid). Over time, the term Moro was eventually embraced as a collective identifier. Most recently, the term is prefixed by the Malay word "Bangsa", which means nation, to suggest a collective name for all Moro peoples in the Philippines and their homeland.

The Bangsamoro diverse culture and identity have been historically recognized in different literatures as basis of identifying and naming the sea between the island of Mindanao and Palawan as Sulu Sea, the country's largest gulf located in the off coast of Mindanao as Moro Gulf and, a large bay of the Moro Gulf, off the southwestern coast of Mindanao island as Ilana Bay, also known as the Iranun/Iranaun Bay. In fact, the island of Mindanao was identified and named as Moro land during the American regime. It was administered separately from the islands of Visayas and Luzon until the promulgation of the 1935 Philippine Constitution despite the vehement resistance of the Bangsamoro.

Bangsamoro culture and identity is also legally recognized through the provision of the 1987 Constitution particularly Article X, Section 15, which provides among others that "There shall be created an Autonomous Region in Muslim Mindanao." Presidential Decree 1083 was decreed allowing the Muslims to exercise their own law on personal and property relation other than the Philippine Civil Code. This led to the creation of Shari'ah legal system including Shari'ah Courts in the country.

Another recognition is the establishment of Ministry of Muslim Affairs (MMA) by virtue of EO 697 issued on May 28, 1981, which was replaced by the Office on Muslim Affairs (OMA) two months later to minimize the number of ministries at that time. It was replaced by the National Commission of Muslim Filipinos (NCMF) by virtue of RA 9997 known as National Commission on Muslim Filipinos Act of 2009. Eventually, the enactment of RA 6734 as amended by RA 9054 and finally eventual passage of RA 11054 known as the BOL.

**Appreciation of Bangsamoro culture and identity is gradually decreasing.** The Bangsamoro are known for their rich and distinct beliefs, culture, and identity for which they fought for and struggled many decades and even centuries before. The battle cry was "it is better to die than to have two identities". This means the Bangsamoro prefer to lose their lives rather than to surrender their identity as Muslim. This battle cry remains in the hearts of the Bangsamoro generation.

However, the Bangsamoro culture and identity that includes arts, sports, music, and others are not much appreciated nor practiced by most of the new generation nowadays due to exposure to foreign cultures, languages, and practices brought about by modern lifestyle and social inclination. They choose to busy themselves with the various social media and its fad than learning their own culture and identity.

**Traditional practices are becoming less popular and less visible.** Thirty years ago, the Bangsamoro traditional practices were common to everyone. On many occasions, the people gathered and feasted on special native delicacies as a gesture of kinship, unity, and solidarity. Indigenous musical instruments were abundant in the Bangsamoro areas. These handcrafted instruments were played during festivals and auspicious occasions. At present, they have become rare and authentic artists are difficult to find. Even worse, majority of the new generation have no knowledge about Bangsamoro history and traditional practices.

Before, the Bangsamoro customary laws of Walay na Bityara in Maguindanaons, Taritib ago Igma of Maranaos, Baitsyara of the people of island provinces and other customs and traditions are observed and effectively used as way of regulating societal affairs. Nowadays, it is very seldom to find someone adept with these customs and traditions.

**Traditional sports, arts, and music slowly forgotten.** With the advent of the modern sports and music coupled with the use of modern gadgets and instruments, the Bangsamoro traditional games, arts, and music are slowly fading and being forgotten. The following are some of the waning Bangsamoro traditional sports and music:

### Table 6.1 Bangsamoro Traditional Sports and Music

| Bangsamoro Traditional Sports | Bangsamoro Traditional Music |
|-------------------------------|------------------------------|
| 1. Kakhoya or local version of Marathon (track and field) | 1. Kakholintang, kaagong, kadbak, kapamamundiran, kaphandaopan |
| 2. Kaphaso or local version of horse racing | 2. Kapangotyapi |
| 3. Kazipa sa Manggis for male | 3. Kapangobing |
| 4. Kazipa sa Lama for female | 4. Kapangintsi |
| 5. Kathakian for boys | |
| 6. Kathipho local version of long jump | |
| 7. Kambalintad for girls | |
| 8. Kambiko for girls and boys | |
| 9. Kanggalawanga | |
| 10. Kangalazoga | |
| 11. Kangugorowa | |

**Bangsamoro historical sites are given less importance thereby losing value and significance.** Due to the absence of consolidated and comprehensive Bangsamoro history; the advent of modernization, and the impact of climate change, the value and significance of the Bangsamoro historical sites are gradually disappearing. This is worsened by the absence of relevant regional policies on the preservation and development of historical sites and cultural heritage.

Under Section 26, Article IX of the BOL, the national government shall transfer the management of Bangsamoro historical and cultural sites currently under the jurisdiction of the National Museum of the Philippines, National Historical Commission of the Philippines, and other agencies of the national government to the Bangsamoro Commission for the Preservation of Cultural Heritage (BCPCH). This will be done through intergovernmental relations mechanisms. The BCPCH shall coordinate with relevant agencies of the national government on the regulation, excavation, and preservation of cultural artifacts and on the recovery of lost historical and cultural heritage.

**Tangible and intangible cultural heritages are vanishing.** Bangsamoro identity through visual and other forms of arts will be extinct sooner than our younger generations might realize. There is but limited knowledge on the historical significance of tangible cultural heritage and properties as well as the intangible cultures of the Bangsamoro. In addition to natural disasters, human-induced calamities threaten the destruction and loss of physical entities related to the uniqueness of the Bangsamoro culture. Being susceptible to transport, most of them were sold, transferred, lost, and gradually forgotten.

---

## Development Opportunities

**Existence of local cultural artists.** Local cultural artists are among the bearers of our cultures through arts. They are the catalysts of the Bangsamoro creative industry which is a growing field in global life. This industry will provide economic growth and decent work for the Bangsamoro cultural artists. In addition, local cultural artists contribute to sustainable tourism that promotes local culture and products. Hence, the remaining existing local cultural artist is a great opportunity in preserving and promoting Bangsamoro culture. They can be utilized as instruments to preserve and develop the cultural arts.

**Presence of cultural arts center.** Cultural centers are spaces for cultural heritage appreciation and artistic growing. It contributes to the efforts to protect and safeguard the Bangsamoro cultural heritage. Moreover, the presence of cultural centers in the region is another opportunity to preserve, develop, and promote culture, and further contribute to sustainable tourism that provides economic opportunities.

**Presence of schools of living tradition (SLT).** In safeguarding intangible cultural heritage, SLT provide a venue to continue the transmission of knowledge to younger generation. It intends to develop traditional cultures such as arts or crafts. It also encourages the culture masters to continue their work in teaching and promoting the cultural heritage and raise more awareness to the community. The presence of SLT in the region provides an opportunity in preserving the Bangsamoro culture.

**Availability of cultural and historical reading material written in local language.** Bangsamoro languages are one of the endangered languages that believed to be lost in the next generation. In order to preserve this culture and its language, it is important to make them available in reading materials. Thus, Bangsamoro languages used in reading materials is an opportunity to transfer the knowledge of the language and culture to the young and the next generations.

---

## Priority Challenges to be Addressed Within 2023-2028

**Declining appreciation and valuation of Bangsamoro cultural heritage.** It is observed that some of the new generations are gradually losing their appreciation and valuation on culture and heritage. This is partly due to the absence of integrated and comprehensive Bangsamoro history that will help them understand the past and the journey of their forefathers. Another factor is poor promotion initiatives in multimedia, lack of reading materials, and other platforms. Thus, the value and appreciation of the Bangsamoro culture and heritage is declining in the region.

**Undermining Bangsamoro identity.** The Bangsamoro is an ethnolinguistic mosaic made up of at least 13 Islamized, non-Islamized, and indigenous groups. As envisioned in the peace agreements, measures to sustain long-term stability shall ensure the inclusion of the minorities, women, and youth. However, low initiatives and learning spaces to engage them in understanding the Bangsamoro history pose a greater challenge, and this weakens the foundation of cultural heritage and identity. Without a strong foundation, it contributes to low interest and awareness of Bangsamoro history and culture. Considering the important role of the youth in the preservation of culture and identity, there is a need to ensure transfer of knowledge and practices to the younger generation who will continue to preserve the Bangsamoro culture and identity.

**Insufficient resources for comprehensive Bangsamoro historical culture and identity.** Although majority of cultural heritage are informal, unprotected, and not explicitly managed, it plays a significant role in both economic and social life because they are the primary institutions that link people. The cultural backdrop of social existence is defined by history, territory, and society. To date, no institution has concentrated on Bangsamoro history intending to build a library of records, objects, and artifacts. Challenges include insufficient and limited inventory and resources encompassing the history, culture, and identity along with the limited number of Bangsamoro historians and cultural experts as well as limited innovative and creative platform to showcase the Bangsamoro culture and identity.

**Weak policies on cultural and historical preservation and innovation.** Currently, the BARMM is adopting the existing national policies on cultural preservation and development. This needs to be localized to promote the distinct culture of the region. Also, weak policies on cultural preservation and promotion can be attributed to limited coordination among BARMM MOAs, poor functionality of local council for culture and arts, non-mainstreaming of culture and identity to governance, lack of cultural product marketing, innovation, and incentives.

---

## Strategic Framework

**Overall Goal:** EMPOWERED, COHESIVE, AND PROGRESSIVE BANGSAMORO

**Sectoral Goal:** RICH AND DIVERSE BANGSAMORO CULTURE AND IDENTITY PRESERVED AND RECOGNIZED

**Sectoral Objective:** 5.1 Cultural identity, diversity, beliefs, and heritage in BARMM protected, preserved, promoted and conserved

**Sub-sector Objectives:**
- 5.1.1 Bangsamoro Cultural Heritage and cohesive identity strengthened
- 5.1.2 Sufficient resources for the development of comprehensive Bangsamoro historical culture and identity ensured
- 5.1.3 Bangsamoro policies on cultural and historical preservation and innovation enacted and implemented
- 5.1.4 Impact of climate change, globalization and migration on cultural heritage addressed and mitigated

**Strategy:** MAINSTREAM BANGSAMORO CULTURAL DIVERSITY, BELIEFS, HERITAGE AND IDENTITY

**Cross-cutting Theme:** GENDER-RESPONSIVE

---

## Development Goal

**Sectoral Goal:** Rich and diverse Bangsamoro culture and identity preserved and recognized.

### Sectoral and Sub-Sector Objectives

**Sectoral objective:** Cultural identity, diversity, beliefs, and heritage in BARMM protected, preserved, promoted, and conserved.

**Sub-sector objectives under this objective target are:**

- Bangsamoro cultural heritage and cohesive identity strengthened;
- Sufficient resources for the development of comprehensive Bangsamoro historical culture and identity ensured;
- Bangsamoro policies on cultural and historical preservation and innovation enacted and implemented; and
- Impact of climate change, globalization and migration on cultural heritage addressed and mitigated.

---

## Priority Strategies and Programs

### Priority Strategies

**Strategy 1:** Preserve and promote Bangsamoro culture and identity through a sustainable transfer of knowledge on cultural skills and practices; and preservation, promotion, and awareness of values formation, cultural identity, diversity, beliefs, and heritage.

**Strategy 2:** Institutionalization of culture-sensitive governance including the provision of innovative platforms through evidence-based research, and passage of laws and policies that protect, preserve, and conserve Bangsamoro identity, beliefs, diverse culture, indigenous games, traditional sports, and heritage.

**Strategy 3:** Promote culture of peace through intensifying information and education campaign.

### Priority Programs

**Bangsamoro Diverse Culture and Identity Promotion Program.** This program is intended to enhance the promotion of Bangsamoro culture and identity. It includes information and education campaign (IEC) through development of IEC materials including Official Gazette of BCPCH "Pusaka" news magazine, Coffee Table Book featuring the rich culture of the Bangsamoro and the conduct of radio programs.

**Bangsamoro Diverse Culture Preservation Program.** This program is designed to intensify the protection and preservation of the Bangsamoro culture and identity. This includes the establishment of SLT, provision of assistance to cultural masters, training on brassware, traditional handicraft and native delicacies making, and indigenous weaving. Also important is the development of curriculum to integrate Bangsamoro culture and history; updating and establishing Bangsamoro Registry Artists, Artisan, and cultural-oriented group; and writing and digitalization of authentic Bangsamoro Tarsila "Jawi" and history.

**Bangsamoro Traditional Sports Promotion Program.** This program aims to preserve and promote the Bangsamoro traditional sports through training of trainers for the different traditional sports and mainstreaming it to the regular sports activities in the BARMM. It also includes formulation and completion of training modules for traditional games.

**Cultural Heritage Protection and Development Program.** This program is geared towards the protection and development of the intangible and tangible cultural heritage of the Bangsamoro. This includes cultural mapping, profiling of intangible and tangible cultural heritage and writing of their short historical significance. This also include construction of necessary infrastructure for the protection and preservation of cultural heritage.

**Islamic Culture Promotion Program.** This program is expected to preserve and promote Islamic culture which has been legally and historically recognized. This includes promotion of culture of peace, strengthening Madrasa system as discussed in Chapter 9, conduct of value transformation training, provision of assistance for the conduct of Hajj (obligatory pilgrimage) and Umrah (voluntary pilgrimage), and the establishment of Shari'ah justice system as discussed in Chapter 5 of this plan.

---

## Priority Policy and Legislative Agenda

**Bangsamoro Heritage Act.** It is a localized version of the RA 10066 that will create a Registry of Cultural Property and preserve tangible and intangible cultural heritage in the BARMM.

---

# Chapter 7: Creating a Favorable Enabling Environment for an Inclusive, Resilient, and Robust Economy

Article V, Section 2 of the BOL states that the Bangsamoro government shall exercise its authority over the following matters without prejudice to the general supervision of the President of the Republic of the Philippines: agriculture, livestock, and food security; barter trade and countertrade; business name registration; cooperatives and social entrepreneurship; economic zones, industrial centers, and free ports; fishery, marine, and aquatic resources; Islamic banking and finance; labor, employment, and occupation; tourism development; and, trade and industry.

This chapter is one of the three chapters covering the economic development sector. This chapter covers only seven of the nine sub-sectors of the economic development sector, namely, agriculture, forestry, fisheries, and agrarian reform; trade and industry; tourism; ecozone development and freeport; investment; cooperatives and social enterprise; and labor and employment.

---

## Sectoral Assessment

### Sectoral Trends

**BARMM has huge potential for economic activities.** It is rich in natural resources that can be used to contribute to the growth of the national economy. The natural terrain of the BARMM is diverse, with lakes, rivers, marshes, mountain ranges, valleys, substantial stretches of fertile land, and maritime resources. However, this huge potential has not been optimized due to the violent conflict that fraught the region, weak institutional support, and the informality that defined the rules governing economic activities for decades.

### Land as an Economic Asset Has Not Been Optimized

A key economic asset that has been left idle and unproductive is land. BARMM has the largest land area in the country, accounting for 11.17 percent of the total land area, and hosts 10 of the 128 Key Biodiversity Areas (KBAs) in the country. About 628,401 hectares are classified as alienable and disposable while 665,151 hectares are classified as forest land. In addition, a total of 340,486 hectares are covered under the Comprehensive Agrarian Reform Program (CARP). These values are the highest in the country. It carries enormous potential as a source of income, food, employment, investment, and housing. But due to the absence of a comprehensive and inclusive land governance framework and the complexity that comes in the plurality of systems (legal, traditional, and informal systems), the potential of land as an economic asset has not been realized. Worse, it has been a significant cause of conflict, death, and displacement of civilians.

In an effort to address land conflict in the region, ministries who are directly and indirectly dealing with lands and its beneficiaries are now converging among themselves to strengthen collaboration with an end to improve delivery of public services while continuing the effort to provide free legal services, especially to agrarian reform beneficiaries and concerned landowners.

Maguindanao has the lion's share of the regional alienable and disposable (A&D) lands at 49 percent, followed by Lanao del Sur at 21 percent. In terms of forest land, Lanao del Sur registered the highest share at 38 percent, followed by Maguindanao at 30 percent. In terms of coverage under the CARP, Lanao del Sur registered the highest share at 48 percent followed by Maguindanao at 28 percent inclusive of Cotabato City and the SGA.

**Figure 7.1 Percent Share to Region, Alienable and Disposable Lands (in hectares), by Province**

| Province | Percent Share | Hectares |
|----------|---------------|----------|
| Maguindanao | 49% | 306,622 ha |
| Lanao del Sur | 21% | - |
| Basilan | 14% | 85,534 ha |
| Tawi-Tawi | 9% | 47,687 ha |
| Sulu | 7% | - |

*Source: DENR*

**Figure 7.2 Percent Share to Region, Forest Land (in hectares), by Province**

| Province | Percent Share | Hectares |
|----------|---------------|----------|
| Lanao del Sur | 38% | - |
| Maguindanao | 30% | 198,138 ha |
| Basilan | 18% | - |
| Tawi-Tawi | 7% | 47,149 ha |
| Sulu | 7% | - |

*Source: DENR*

### High Rate of Contested Lands in BARMM

About 16 percent or 213,196.17 hectares of the land area are considered contested. 57 percent of the contested lands are in Lanao del Sur, accounting for 28.7 percent of the provincial land. Maguindanao has the second-highest number of contested lands, accounting for 31 percent of the total land area in BARMM and 13.15 percent of the provincial land.

**Figure 7.3 Percent Share to Region, Contested vs. Uncontested Lands (in hectares), by BARMM**

| Category | Percent Share | Hectares |
|----------|---------------|----------|
| Uncontested Areas | 84% | 1,143,599 ha |
| Contested Areas | 16% | - |

*Source: DENR*

**Figure 7.4 Percent Share to Region, Contested Lands (in hectares), by Province**

| Province | Percent Share | Hectares |
|----------|---------------|----------|
| Lanao del Sur | 57% | 121,147.0597 ha |
| Maguindanao | 31% | - |
| Basilan | 10% | 20,374.6 ha |
| Tawi-Tawi | 2% | - |
| Sulu | 0% | - |

*Source: DENR*

### Gaps Persist in Land Distribution and Tenurial Security

From the total CARP scope of 340,486.8532 hectares as of December 2021, only 68 percent or 229,972.9852 hectares had been distributed under the CARP. This means that 110,513.8680 hectares are yet to be distributed. Lanao del Sur registered the highest distribution rate at 82 percent, followed by Basilan at 68 percent while Maguindanao posted the lowest rate of distribution at 48 percent.

### Table 7.1 Land Acquisitions and Distribution Data, as of October 3, 2019

| Province | Global CARP Scope (in hectares) | Cumulative Accomp/Land Distributed | Rate of Distribution (%) | Balance Against Scope (%) | Land Balances | Number of ARBs |
|----------|--------------------------------|-----------------------------------|-------------------------|--------------------------|---------------|----------------|
| Basilan | 37,115.6185 | 25,217.2925 | 68 | 32 | 11,898.326 | 8,089.0000 |
| Lanao del Sur | 164,538.5787 | 135,449.4746 | 82 | 18 | 29,089.1041 | 34,757 |
| Maguindanao | 95,183.7423 | 45,438.3968 | 48 | 52 | 49,745.3455 | 15,877.0000 |
| Sulu | 20,025.9837 | 9,874.9704 | 49 | 51 | 10,151.0133 | 3,508.0000 |
| Tawi-Tawi | 21,965.41 | 13,850.9 | 64 | 36 | 7,972.5591 | 3,148 |
| SGA | 1,657.52 | 0 | 0 | 100 | 1,657.52 | 0 |
| **BARMM** | **340,486.8523** | **229,972.9852** | **68** | **32** | **110,513.868** | **65,379.0000** |

*Source: MAFAR*

In terms of the provision of support services, a total of 104 Agrarian Reform Communities (ARCs) have been established, launched, and are able to organize a total of 240 Agrarian Reform Beneficiary Organizations (ARBOs). The formation of ARCs and ARBOs is one of the strategies of the program to help maximize the chances of Agrarian Reform Beneficiaries (ARBs) to avail interventions both local and foreign assisted. It is a fact that most of the interventions especially from foreign donors are course through cooperatives or organized groups, thus the formation of ARCs and ARBOs. A total of 34,743 ARBs have been assisted as of October 2019. Support includes: training (soft component) and the delivery of farm input support, common service facilities, and infrastructures (hard component).

### Table 7.2 Agrarian Reform Communities and Agrarian Reform Beneficiary Organizations Data

**Total Members in ARBO, as of October 3, 2019**

| Province | No. of ARC | No. of ARBO | No. of ARB Member | No. of Non-ARB Member | Total No. of Members |
|----------|------------|-------------|-------------------|----------------------|---------------------|
| Basilan | 11 | 18 | 5,640 | 0 | 5,640 |
| Sulu | 12 | 25 | 412 | 1,885 | 2,297 |
| Tawi-Tawi | 18 | 28 | 1,347 | 0 | 1,347 |
| Maguindanao | 26 | 43 | 14,043 | 0 | 14,043 |
| Lanao del Sur | 37 | 129 | 9,371 | 2,045 | 11,416 |
| **BARMM** | **104** | **240** | **30,813** | **3,930** | **34,743** |

*Source: MAFAR*

**Agriculture and fishery are BARMM's main sources of food, employment, and income.** It used to be the largest contributor to the region's overall economy 21 years ago, accounting for more than half of the region's economy. With the growth of the services sector, it is now the second-highest contributor to the region's economy, accounting for 36.41 percent in 2021. This structural transformation, however, does not mean that the sector is weakening or becoming less relevant. In fact, it is one of the biggest contributors that caused the region to be resilient at the time of the pandemic, registering the lowest decline among the regions in the Philippines in 2020.

### Seaweeds as BARMM's Top Commodity

BARMM is the country's largest seaweed producer, accounting for 48.42 percent of the country's seaweed production. Tawi-Tawi is the main source of seaweed production in BARMM, registering more than 90 percent of regional production. The municipalities in Sitangkai and Sibutu islands are the largest seaweed culture areas in Tawi-Tawi province. The comparative advantage and market potential for carrageenan as an export product of seaweeds remains high in the region.

To sustain the seaweed production, BARMM must invest in the post-harvest processing plant and modernized facilities/equipment or the overall value-chain development of the seaweeds industry, establish municipal-wide seaweed nursery, and strengthen seaweed research and development in order to have full control from production to marketing.

### High-Value Crops on the Rise in BARMM

Valued by small-scale farmers for its tolerance and growth, cassava is the second top commodity of BARMM based on percent share, accounting for 41.7 percent of the country's total cassava production. As a complement or alternative to rice and corn as a staple food, cassava was ranked significantly high in terms of volume of production and area harvested. Its yield in 2020 was close to the national average. Lanao del Sur was the largest contributor to regional cassava production in 2020, accounting for 47 percent.

BARMM is the country's second-largest producer of mangosteen, accounting for 22.4 percent of the country's production. It is the country's third largest producer of rubber, coffee, lanzones, and corn, contributing 18.4 percent, 17.4 percent, 15.3 percent, and 14 percent, respectively. In addition, the region is the second-largest producer of durian and the 5th largest producer of abaca.

### Table 7.3 BARMM Top 10 Commodities, by Percent Share, 2020

| Commodity | Percent Share to National Production (%) | Rank (in terms of percent share to national production) |
|-----------|------------------------------------------|--------------------------------------------------------|
| Seaweed | 48.42 | 1 |
| Cassava | 41.70 | 1 |
| Mangosteen | 22.40 | 2 |
| Rubber | 18.40 | 3 |
| Coffee | 17.40 | 3 |
| Lanzones | 15.30 | 3 |
| Corn | 14.00 | 3 |
| Coconut | 9.30 | 5 |
| Durian | 8.40 | 2 |
| Mung Bean | 7.10 | 5 |

*Source: PSA*

### BARMM Has Higher Productivity Than the National Average in Six Out of 10 Leading Commodities

Rubber, coffee, coconut, durian, mung bean, and abaca are the six commodities in which BARMM registered higher productivity than the national average. On the other hand, cassava, mangosteen, lanzones, and corn are the commodities in which BARMM registered lower productivity than the national average.

**Figure 7.5 Yield (metric ton/hectare), 10 top commodities, BARMM vs. National, 2020**

|-----------|-------------|----------------|

| Rubber | 4.75 | 4.09 |
| Coffee | 3.97 | 3.11 |
| Lanzones | 1.33 | 1.99 |
| Corn | 1.83 | - |
| Coconut | 0.96 | 0.54 |
| Durian | 1.54 | 0.88 |
| Mung Bean | 0.76 | 0.74 |
| Abaca | 0.60 | 0.45 |

*Source: PSA*

Mangosteen is BARMM's third top commodity, accounting for 22.4 percent of the country's volume of production and 30.9 percent in terms of area harvested. Sulu is the largest producer of mangosteen in the region. Rubber comes fourth, with Basilan as the largest producer in the region. The region's yield of rubber was higher than the national average.

### Table 7.4 Priority Commodities by Province

| Province | Priority Commodities |
|----------|---------------------|
| Maguindanao | Rubber, coffee, coconut, banana, goat |
| Lanao del Sur | Cassava, coconut, banana, abaca, coffee |

| Sulu | Coffee, cassava, abaca, seaweeds, coconut |

*Source: MAFAR*

### Bangsamoro Strategic Agricultural and Fisheries Development Areas (BSAFDA)

The BSAFDA was identified as the essential supporting strategy in the attainment of various development goals and priority agenda in the region, namely: zero hunger; sustainable agri-fishery production; consumption; and food security.

All identified agri-fishery and agro-industrial areas clustered under BSAFDA in the Bangsamoro region shall form part of the Network of Protected Areas for Fishery, Agricultural and Agro-Fishery Industrial Development (NPAFAAD) to avoid illegal land use conversions and to protect the welfare of the farmers and as well as the Bangsamoro people through food security and sustainable agri-fishery production and agro-industrial establishment in achieving the Bangsamoro that is socioeconomically stable, resilient and globally competitive farmers and fisherfolks.

Currently, the identified BSAFDAs cover about 628,872.64 hectares for agricultural production and 125,562.44 for inland fishing as shown in Table 7.5 below. Maguindanao del Sur has the largest agriculture and inland fishery areas included in the protected networks.

### Table 7.5 BSAFDAs Covered Areas (in Hectares), BARMM, 2022

|----------|-------------|------------------|

| Maguindanao del Norte | 124,346.99 | 23,067.33 |
| Lanao del Sur | 135,052.82 | 37,779.69 |
| Basilan | 80,084.38 | 1,757.61 |
| Sulu | 89,097.99 | 2,435.64 |
| Tawi-Tawi | 22,153.22 | 682.90 |

*Source: Ministry of Agriculture Fisheries and Agrarian Reform (MAFAR); Prepared by: Bangsamoro Planning and Development Authority - Map Team (BPDA-Map Team)*

### Aquaculture as the Biggest Source of Fisheries Production in BARMM

The total volume of production of BARMM for fisheries in 2021 is 1,010,804.29 mt. Tawi-Tawi has the highest volume of production with 442,052.47 mt., followed by Sulu with 333,145.38 mt., Maguindanao with 196,294.21 mt., and Basilan with 23,396.75 mt. Meanwhile, Lanao del Sur recorded 15,915.47 mt.

**Figure 7.8 Volume of Fisheries Production in BARMM, by Province: 2021 (in metric tons)**

| Province | Percent Share | Volume (mt) |
|----------|---------------|-------------|
| Tawi-Tawi | 44% | 442,052.47 |
| Sulu | 33% | 333,145.38 |
| Maguindanao | 19% | 196,294.21 |
| Basilan | 2% | 23,396.75 |
| Lanao del Sur | 2% | 15,915.47 |

*Source: PSA*

About 79.89 percent of the total volume of fisheries production in BARMM was from Aquaculture. This is followed by Municipal Fisheries and Commercial Fisheries (MFCF) comprising 12.07 percent and 8.03 percent, respectively. Production in all subsectors increased by corresponding growth rates of 3.69 percent in municipal fisheries and 1.33 percent in aquaculture while the commercial fisheries decreased with a rate of 0.22 percent. Municipal fisheries are composed of marine fisheries (82.71 percent) and inland fisheries (20.90 percent).

**Figure 7.9 Distribution of Volume of Fisheries Production in BARMM, by Subsector: Q1 2021**

| Subsector | Percent Share |
|-----------|---------------|
| Aquaculture | 79.89% |
| Municipal Fisheries | 12.07% |
| Commercial Fisheries | 8.03% |

*Source: PSA*

About 92.70 percent of the total volume of fisheries production in BARMM was composed of the top 10 species during the first quarter of 2021. Seaweed posted the highest level of production in the region during this period. Its production increased by 2.56 percent between the first quarters of 2020 and 2021. This was followed by Frigate tuna (Tulingan), Roundscad (Galunggong), Big-eyed scad (Matangbaka) and Eastern little tuna (Bonito).

**Figure 7.10 Volume of Top 10 Fisheries Production in BARMM, by Species: Q1 2020 - Q1 2021 (in metric tons)**

| Species | Q1 2021 | Q1 2020 |
|---------|---------|---------|
| Seaweed | 216,797.58 | - |
| Frigate tuna (Tulingan) | 7,102.28 | - |
| Roundscad (Galunggong) | 6,020.18 | - |
| Big-eyed scad (Matangbaka) | 4,851.36 | - |
| Eastern little tuna (Bonito) | 3,693.29 | - |
| Tilapia | 3,425.15 | - |
| Indian mackerel (Alumahan) | 2,895.43 | - |

| Yellowfin tuna (Tambakol/Bariles) | 3,433.49 | - |
| Skipjack (Gulyasan) | 2,587.48 | - |

*Source: PSA*

In 2021, the volume of production for aquaculture is 737,640.40 mt. with a value of production of Php7,100,856.41 while commercial has 95,967.00 mt. volume of production and Php3,492,638.30 value of production.

For the municipal, inland accounts for 68,895.81 mt. volume of production and Php7,571,879.88 value of production. Whereas, the marine accounts for 108,301.08 mt. volume of production and Php6,765,750.67 value of production.

### Table 7.6 Aquaculture, Commercial, and Municipal Volume and Value of Production

| Fishing | Volume of Production (mt.) | Value of Production (in Php) |
|---------|---------------------------|------------------------------|
| Aquaculture | 737,640.40 | 7,100,856.41 |
| Commercial | 95,967.00 | 3,492,638.30 |
| Municipal - Inland | 68,895.81 | 7,571,879.88 |
| Municipal - Marine | 108,301.08 | 6,765,750.67 |

*Source: PSA*

The region has 113 municipalities, 52 of these are coastal municipalities and 45 are inland municipalities. The second largest lake in the country is found in Lanao del Sur with an approximate area of 34,700 hectares. Lake Lanao is the deepest in the country and is considered one of the major tropical lakes in Southeast Asia. It is the source of hydroelectric power for the whole island of Mindanao aside from providing irrigation water and various fish products. Other lakes like Dapao in Lanao del Sur, Buluan and Balut in Maguindanao, Seit and Panamao in Sulu, Danao and Singuang in Mapun, Tawi-Tawi, and similar bodies of water are also potential resources of commercial scale fish products. The region has the largest share of swamp area with around 105,000 hectares and is being programmed for extensive agricultural and fishery development.

The region has the highest registered fisherfolk population in the country. As of December 2018, a total of 252,758 fisherfolks were registered in the Fisherfolks Registration (FishR) system. Of these, 162,167 are male and 90,951 are female; 102,996 are engaged in capture fishing; 45,275 are in aquaculture; 17,168 in fish vending; 23,251 in gleaning; 9,363 in fish processing; and 16,765 are engaged in other types of fishing activities.

For the period of 2014 up to 2018, fisheries production increased every year except in 2016 and 2017 due to typhoons and dry spells that affected the production of commercial and municipal fish products. Aquaculture has, however, continuously increased.

### Table 7.7 Fisheries, Volume of Production, by Geolocation, Subsector, 2015-2021

|----------|------|------|------|------|------|------|------|

In terms of fisheries volume of production by geolocation (commercial, municipal, marine municipal, inland municipal, and aquaculture fisheries), the region had an aggregate of 993,464.83 mt.
